###xml 755 762 755 762 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="table" rid="T4">Table 4</xref>
###xml 237 244 <span type="species:ncbi:562">E. coli</span>
###xml 249 260 <span type="species:ncbi:1423">B. subtilis</span>
###xml 320 331 <span type="species:ncbi:1423">B. subtilis</span>
###xml 417 428 <span type="species:ncbi:1423">B. subtilis</span>
###xml 434 441 <span type="species:ncbi:562">E. coli</span>
###xml 536 547 <span type="species:ncbi:1423">B. subtilis</span>
###xml 561 568 <span type="species:ncbi:562">E. coli</span>
###xml 615 626 <span type="species:ncbi:1423">B. subtilis</span>
Rates in the RNase P holoenzyme reactions were measured under single turnover conditions at pH 6.0 and 4 mM Mg2+, or at 10 mM Mg2+ for the most inhibitory LNA and 2'-OCH3 modifications. Effects were similar in reactions catalyzed by the E. coli and B. subtilis holoenzymes, with two major exceptions: (i) similar to the B. subtilis RNA-alone reaction, the defect caused by the 2'-H (C-1) ptRNA was much higher in the B. subtilis than E. coli holoenzyme reaction; (ii) weak cleavage of the 2'-OCH3 substrate at the c0 site occurred with B. subtilis but not with E. coli RNase P, as mentioned before. As observed for B. subtilis P RNA (see above), the extent of cleavage for the LNA (T-1) and 2'-OCH3 (T-1) substrates by both holoenzymes was extremely low (Table 4).
###end p 59

###begin p 61
###xml 57 58 57 58 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">0</sub>
###xml 64 66 64 66 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+1</sub>
###xml 75 82 75 82 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">E. coli</italic>
###xml 90 98 90 98 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F2">Figure 2</xref>
###xml 200 202 200 202 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B14">14</xref>

###xml 1402 1410 1402 1410 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F8">Scheme 1</xref>

###xml 1487 1488 1487 1488 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">0</sub>
###xml 1524 1526 1524 1526 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+1</sub>
###xml 1546 1548 1546 1548 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">2+</sup>
###xml 1597 1599 1597 1599 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">2+</sup>
###xml 1602 1610 1602 1610 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F4">Figure 4</xref>
###xml 75 82 <span type="species:ncbi:562">E. coli</span>
###xml 668 672 <span type="species:ncbi:59530">kobs</span>
###xml 971 975 <span type="species:ncbi:59530">kobs</span>
###xml 1477 1481 <span type="species:ncbi:59530">kobs</span>
The LNA (T-1) ptRNA was simultaneously processed at the c0 and m+1 site by E. coli P RNA (Figure 2B and C). Simultaneous processing at different sites can be attributed to parallel reaction pathways (14,19,22,23,41) as illustrated in Scheme 1. Here we investigated the Mg2+ dependence of c0 and m+1 cleavage, with the following outcome: First, at low [Mg2+], the fraction of substrate cleaved at the c0 site (Fc) was substantially lower at early versus late time points (Figure 4A); with increasing [Mg2+], these differences largely disappeared (Figure 4B-D). This indicates that the two (E*S) complexes reacted with different single exponential decay rate constants (kobs) at low [Mg2+] and similar rate constants at high [Mg2+]. Second, Fc values at experimental endpoints (last measured time point) and theoretical endpoints (derived from the single exponential decay curve fit) were lower at 20 mM Mg2+ (Fc < 0.7) than at e.g. 120 mM Mg2+ (Fc > 0.8). Third, plotting kobs as a function of [Mg2+] (Figure 4E) and allowing for a cooperative Mg2+ dependence at both cleavage sites (see Materials and methods section) revealed a higher Mg2+ dependence for cleavage at the c0 site (K1/2 = 184 mM) than at the aberrant m+1 site (K1/2 = 129 mM), consistent with a severe metal ion binding defect for c0 cleavage in the presence of LNA at nt -1 (the defect may include contributions from Kconf or kc0; see Scheme 1). A double logarithmic plot of the same data illustrates that the kobs for c0 cleavage was slower than that for m+1 cleavage at low [Mg2+], whereas this relation was reversed at high [Mg2+] (Figure 4F).
###end p 61

###xml 78 82 <span type="species:ncbi:59530">kobs</span>
###xml 453 457 <span type="species:ncbi:59530">kobs</span>
###xml 990 997 <span type="species:ncbi:562">E. coli</span>
###xml 1352 1356 <span type="species:ncbi:59530">kobs</span>
In the hydrolysis reaction catalyzed by RNase P, a linear relationship of log kobs and pH with a slope of approximately1 is taken as evidence for the catalytic step being rate-limiting (8,9,11,20,22,42). This pH dependence, consistent with a single ionization, is thought to reflect ionization of a metal ion-coordinated water molecule acting as the nucleophile in this phosphodiester hydrolysis reaction (8,11,22). Analysis of the pH dependence of log kobs for cleavage of the LNA (T-1) ptRNA at sites c0 and m+1 (Figure 5) revealed a slope of approximately0.9 up to pH 7 for c0 cleavage, after which the curve inflected (Figure 5D), consistent with another step limiting the rate of the reaction above pH 7 (9). In contrast, cleavage at the m+1 site was linear over the entire pH range (5.5-8.1) but with a slope of only 0.45 (Figure 5D), indicative of another step than cleavage chemistry limiting the reaction at all tested pH values. Figure 5.Kinetics of ptRNA LNA (T-1) processing by E. coli P RNA at different pH values. Cleavage experiments were performed under single turnover conditions (5 microM P RNA, <1 nM ptRNA) in the presence of 1 M NH4OAc, 50 mM MES, PIPES or HEPES buffer and 60 mM Mg2+. (A-C) Examples of kinetic analyses at low, intermediate and the highest pH tested. For further details, see legend to Figure 4. (D) Plots of log kobs dependence on pH. For cleavage at the c0 site (open circles), the first four data points gave a linear fit with a slope of 0.92 +/- 0.08; for cleavage at the m+1 site (filled triangles), all five data points fit to a line with a slope of 0.45 +/- 0.04.
###end p 63

###xml 404 408 <span type="species:ncbi:59530">kobs</span>
Kinetics of ptRNA LNA (T-1) processing by E. coli P RNA at different pH values. Cleavage experiments were performed under single turnover conditions (5 microM P RNA, <1 nM ptRNA) in the presence of 1 M NH4OAc, 50 mM MES, PIPES or HEPES buffer and 60 mM Mg2+. (A-C) Examples of kinetic analyses at low, intermediate and the highest pH tested. For further details, see legend to Figure 4. (D) Plots of log kobs dependence on pH. For cleavage at the c0 site (open circles), the first four data points gave a linear fit with a slope of 0.92 +/- 0.08; for cleavage at the m+1 site (filled triangles), all five data points fit to a line with a slope of 0.45 +/- 0.04.
###end p 64
###begin p 65
###xml 65 67 65 67 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">2+</sup>
###xml 163 165 163 165 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">2+</sup>
###xml 195 203 195 203 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F4">Figure 4</xref>
###xml 218 220 218 220 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+1</sub>
###xml 247 248 247 248 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">0</sub>
###xml 304 312 304 312 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F5">Figure 5</xref>
###xml 363 371 363 371 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F5">Figure 5</xref>
###xml 418 419 418 419 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">0</sub>
###xml 426 427 426 427 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">F</italic>
###xml 427 428 427 428 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">c</sub>
The pH variation experiments were performed at an intermediate Mg2+ concentration (60 mM). Interestingly, lowering the pH caused similar effects as lowering the Mg2+ concentration at neutral pH (Figure 4): at low pH, m+1 cleavage was faster than c0 cleavage, while this relation was inversed above pH 7 (Figure 5D); and, related to this, at low versus higher pH (Figure 5A-C) the fraction of substrate cleaved at the c0 site (Fc) was substantially lower at early than late time points.
###end p 65
###begin title 66
DISCUSSION
###end title 66
###begin title 67
###xml 14 15 14 15 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">3</sub>
LNA and 2'-OCH3 modifications at nt -1: blockage of steps after substrate ground state binding
###end title 67
###begin p 68
###xml 8 9 8 9 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">3</sub>
###xml 144 151 144 151 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">E. coli</italic>
###xml 159 160 159 160 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B8">8</xref>
###xml 256 258 256 258 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+1</sub>
###xml 341 342 341 342 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">&#8722;</sub>
###xml 342 343 342 343 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">1</sub>
###xml 413 414 413 414 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">3</sub>
###xml 427 434 427 434 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">E. coli</italic>
###xml 497 505 497 505 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F2">Figure 2</xref>
###xml 541 542 541 542 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">3</sub>
###xml 657 658 657 658 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B8">8</xref>
###xml 144 151 <span type="species:ncbi:562">E. coli</span>
###xml 427 434 <span type="species:ncbi:562">E. coli</span>
A 2'-OCH3 modification at position -1 was previously reported as the most inhibitory substrate ribose modification in the reaction catalyzed by E. coli P RNA (8). In this previous study, a ptRNA with a single nucleotide as 5'-leader was miscleaved at the m+1 site as observed here. The substrate with a 1-nt 5'-leader precluded analysis of m-1 cleavage, but as shown here this site appears to be blocked by 2'-OCH3 at nt -1 in E. coli P RNA-alone reactions with ptRNAs carrying longer 5'-leaders (Figure 2). The inhibitory strength of 2'-OCH3 at -1 exceeded that of a 2'-deoxy modification, consistent with a steric effect caused by the extra methyl group (8).
###end p 68
###begin p 69
###xml 26 33 26 33 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">E. coli</italic>

###xml 2404 2405 2404 2405 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">B</sub>
###xml 2433 2438 2433 2438 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">pro-R</italic>

###xml 2818 2820 2818 2820 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B26">26</xref>
###xml 2824 2825 2824 2825 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">B</bold>

###xml 2963 2964 2963 2964 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">3</sub>
###xml 3057 3058 3057 3058 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">B</sub>
###xml 3097 3099 3097 3099 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B56">56</xref>
###xml 3192 3193 3192 3193 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">B</sub>
###xml 3242 3247 3242 3247 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">pro-R</italic>
###xml 3388 3390 3388 3390 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B56">56</xref>
###xml 3716 3727 3716 3727 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Azoarcus sp</italic>
###xml 3734 3737 3734 3737 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Ile</sup>
###xml 3754 3756 3754 3756 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B57">57</xref>
###xml 3802 3803 3802 3803 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">B</sub>
###xml 4057 4059 4057 4059 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B57">57</xref>
###xml 4194 4205 4194 4205 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Tetrahymena</italic>
###xml 4242 4249 4242 4249 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">E. coli</italic>
###xml 4505 4516 4505 4516 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Tetrahymena</italic>
###xml 4652 4654 4652 4654 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B20">20</xref>
###xml 4655 4657 4655 4657 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B58">58</xref>
###xml 2185 4723 2185 4723 <p xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" textid="70">Transition state models for <italic>E. coli</italic> RNase P RNA-catalyzed phosphodiester hydrolysis at the canonical c<sub>0</sub> site with a ribose (<bold>A</bold>) or LNA (<bold>B</bold>) at nt &#8722;1. The model in (A) [adapted from ref. (<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="B55">55</xref>)] predicts that both Mg<sub>A</sub> and Mg<sub>B</sub> directly coordinate to the <italic>pro-R</italic>p oxygen, with Mg<sub>A</sub> coordinating the OH<sup>&#8211;</sup> nucleophile, and Mg<sub>B</sub> interacting with the 3&#8242;-oxygen and the 2&#8242;-hydroxyl group at nt &#8722;1 via a water molecule of its hydration shell; we propose this water molecule to donate the proton to the 3&#8242;-oxyanion leaving group. The ribose at nt &#8722;1 is assumed to mainly populate the 2&#8242;-<italic>endo</italic> sugar pucker based on NMR analyses of acceptor stem mimics (<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="B26">26</xref>). (<bold>B</bold>) Here, the &#8722;1 nucleotide is LNA with the sugar moiety fixed in the C3&#8242;-<italic>endo</italic> conformation; the steric interference caused by LNA or 2&#8242;-OCH<sub>3</sub> at position &#8722;1 (indicated by the dashed curved line) likely disrupts the binding site for Mg<sub>B</sub>. The model differs from others [e.g. (<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="B56">56</xref>)] by assuming that only two instead of three metal ions are essential; also, in our model Mg<sub>B</sub> is proposed to simultaneously interact with the <italic>pro-R</italic>p oxygen, the 3&#8242;-oxygen and the 2&#8242;-OH, whereas the latter interaction is assigned to the third metal ion in the model by Kazantsev and Pace (<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="B56">56</xref>). Group I intron ribozymes have a similar size as bacterial P RNA and also catalyze phosphoryl transfer reactions yielding products with 3&#8242;-OH termini. The two-metal-ion mechanistic model we propose here is essentially identical to that for the second step of group I intron splicing inferred from a crystal structure of the <italic>Azoarcus sp</italic>. ptRNA<sup>Ile</sup> group I intron (<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="B57">57</xref>), with two differences: (i) the contact of Mg<sub>B</sub> to the 2&#8242;-OH is outer-sphere in the RNase P reaction, but inner-sphere in the group I intron reaction; (ii) the nucleophile is a hydroxide in the RNase P reaction, but the terminal 3&#8242;-OH of the 5&#8242;-exon in the second step of the group I intron reaction (<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="B57">57</xref>). Despite striking similarities, one mechanistic difference has been observed in phosphodiester hydrolysis reactions catalyzed by the <italic>Tetrahymena</italic> group I intron-derived ribozyme and <italic>E. coli</italic> P RNA: whereas a 2&#8242;-amino modification at ptRNA position &#8722;1 inhibited cleavage at the canonical site with increasing protonation of the 2&#8242;-amine in the P RNA reaction (consistent with electrostatic repulsion of a metal ion), the reverse was seen with the <italic>Tetrahymena</italic> ribozyme; in the latter reaction, protonation of the 2&#8242;-amine at equivalent position rather stimulated cleavage at the canonical site (<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="B20">20</xref>,<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="B58">58</xref>). The molecular basis of this difference is not clear at present.</p>
###xml 2185 4723 2185 4723 <caption xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><p textid="70">Transition state models for <italic>E. coli</italic> RNase P RNA-catalyzed phosphodiester hydrolysis at the canonical c<sub>0</sub> site with a ribose (<bold>A</bold>) or LNA (<bold>B</bold>) at nt &#8722;1. The model in (A) [adapted from ref. (<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="B55">55</xref>)] predicts that both Mg<sub>A</sub> and Mg<sub>B</sub> directly coordinate to the <italic>pro-R</italic>p oxygen, with Mg<sub>A</sub> coordinating the OH<sup>&#8211;</sup> nucleophile, and Mg<sub>B</sub> interacting with the 3&#8242;-oxygen and the 2&#8242;-hydroxyl group at nt &#8722;1 via a water molecule of its hydration shell; we propose this water molecule to donate the proton to the 3&#8242;-oxyanion leaving group. The ribose at nt &#8722;1 is assumed to mainly populate the 2&#8242;-<italic>endo</italic> sugar pucker based on NMR analyses of acceptor stem mimics (<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="B26">26</xref>). (<bold>B</bold>) Here, the &#8722;1 nucleotide is LNA with the sugar moiety fixed in the C3&#8242;-<italic>endo</italic> conformation; the steric interference caused by LNA or 2&#8242;-OCH<sub>3</sub> at position &#8722;1 (indicated by the dashed curved line) likely disrupts the binding site for Mg<sub>B</sub>. The model differs from others [e.g. (<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="B56">56</xref>)] by assuming that only two instead of three metal ions are essential; also, in our model Mg<sub>B</sub> is proposed to simultaneously interact with the <italic>pro-R</italic>p oxygen, the 3&#8242;-oxygen and the 2&#8242;-OH, whereas the latter interaction is assigned to the third metal ion in the model by Kazantsev and Pace (<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="B56">56</xref>). Group I intron ribozymes have a similar size as bacterial P RNA and also catalyze phosphoryl transfer reactions yielding products with 3&#8242;-OH termini. The two-metal-ion mechanistic model we propose here is essentially identical to that for the second step of group I intron splicing inferred from a crystal structure of the <italic>Azoarcus sp</italic>. ptRNA<sup>Ile</sup> group I intron (<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="B57">57</xref>), with two differences: (i) the contact of Mg<sub>B</sub> to the 2&#8242;-OH is outer-sphere in the RNase P reaction, but inner-sphere in the group I intron reaction; (ii) the nucleophile is a hydroxide in the RNase P reaction, but the terminal 3&#8242;-OH of the 5&#8242;-exon in the second step of the group I intron reaction (<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="B57">57</xref>). Despite striking similarities, one mechanistic difference has been observed in phosphodiester hydrolysis reactions catalyzed by the <italic>Tetrahymena</italic> group I intron-derived ribozyme and <italic>E. coli</italic> P RNA: whereas a 2&#8242;-amino modification at ptRNA position &#8722;1 inhibited cleavage at the canonical site with increasing protonation of the 2&#8242;-amine in the P RNA reaction (consistent with electrostatic repulsion of a metal ion), the reverse was seen with the <italic>Tetrahymena</italic> ribozyme; in the latter reaction, protonation of the 2&#8242;-amine at equivalent position rather stimulated cleavage at the canonical site (<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="B20">20</xref>,<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="B58">58</xref>). The molecular basis of this difference is not clear at present.</p></caption>
###xml 4723 4723 4723 4723 <graphic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xlink:href="gkp775f6"/>
###xml 2176 4723 2176 4723 <fig xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" id="F6" position="float"><label>Figure 6.</label><caption><p textid="70">Transition state models for <italic>E. coli</italic> RNase P RNA-catalyzed phosphodiester hydrolysis at the canonical c<sub>0</sub> site with a ribose (<bold>A</bold>) or LNA (<bold>B</bold>) at nt &#8722;1. The model in (A) [adapted from ref. (<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="B55">55</xref>)] predicts that both Mg<sub>A</sub> and Mg<sub>B</sub> directly coordinate to the <italic>pro-R</italic>p oxygen, with Mg<sub>A</sub> coordinating the OH<sup>&#8211;</sup> nucleophile, and Mg<sub>B</sub> interacting with the 3&#8242;-oxygen and the 2&#8242;-hydroxyl group at nt &#8722;1 via a water molecule of its hydration shell; we propose this water molecule to donate the proton to the 3&#8242;-oxyanion leaving group. The ribose at nt &#8722;1 is assumed to mainly populate the 2&#8242;-<italic>endo</italic> sugar pucker based on NMR analyses of acceptor stem mimics (<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="B26">26</xref>). (<bold>B</bold>) Here, the &#8722;1 nucleotide is LNA with the sugar moiety fixed in the C3&#8242;-<italic>endo</italic> conformation; the steric interference caused by LNA or 2&#8242;-OCH<sub>3</sub> at position &#8722;1 (indicated by the dashed curved line) likely disrupts the binding site for Mg<sub>B</sub>. The model differs from others [e.g. (<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="B56">56</xref>)] by assuming that only two instead of three metal ions are essential; also, in our model Mg<sub>B</sub> is proposed to simultaneously interact with the <italic>pro-R</italic>p oxygen, the 3&#8242;-oxygen and the 2&#8242;-OH, whereas the latter interaction is assigned to the third metal ion in the model by Kazantsev and Pace (<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="B56">56</xref>). Group I intron ribozymes have a similar size as bacterial P RNA and also catalyze phosphoryl transfer reactions yielding products with 3&#8242;-OH termini. The two-metal-ion mechanistic model we propose here is essentially identical to that for the second step of group I intron splicing inferred from a crystal structure of the <italic>Azoarcus sp</italic>. ptRNA<sup>Ile</sup> group I intron (<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="B57">57</xref>), with two differences: (i) the contact of Mg<sub>B</sub> to the 2&#8242;-OH is outer-sphere in the RNase P reaction, but inner-sphere in the group I intron reaction; (ii) the nucleophile is a hydroxide in the RNase P reaction, but the terminal 3&#8242;-OH of the 5&#8242;-exon in the second step of the group I intron reaction (<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="B57">57</xref>). Despite striking similarities, one mechanistic difference has been observed in phosphodiester hydrolysis reactions catalyzed by the <italic>Tetrahymena</italic> group I intron-derived ribozyme and <italic>E. coli</italic> P RNA: whereas a 2&#8242;-amino modification at ptRNA position &#8722;1 inhibited cleavage at the canonical site with increasing protonation of the 2&#8242;-amine in the P RNA reaction (consistent with electrostatic repulsion of a metal ion), the reverse was seen with the <italic>Tetrahymena</italic> ribozyme; in the latter reaction, protonation of the 2&#8242;-amine at equivalent position rather stimulated cleavage at the canonical site (<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="B20">20</xref>,<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="B58">58</xref>). The molecular basis of this difference is not clear at present.</p></caption><graphic xlink:href="gkp775f6"/></fig>
###xml 26 33 <span type="species:ncbi:562">E. coli</span>
###xml 1133 1140 <span type="species:ncbi:562">E. coli</span>
###xml 2213 2220 <span type="species:ncbi:562">E. coli</span>
###xml 4242 4249 <span type="species:ncbi:562">E. coli</span>
Our results obtained with E. coli P RNA demonstrate that the transition state for canonical cleavage can be achieved in the presence of a fixed C3'-endo conformation at nt -1 (Figure 2B and C), indicating that a C2'-endo conformation prevailing at this position based on NMR analyses (26) is not an absolute requirement for catalysis to occur. It is unclear if the low efficiency of c0 cleavage with LNA at -1 is primarily due to the steric effect of the extra methylene group or includes substantial contributions from the lack of ribose flexibility at this position. Also, the strong inhibitory effect of LNA might stem from the fact that the free electron pairs of the 2'-oxygen in LNA are orientationally constrained; this may impair the correct positioning of the MgB-hydrate complex containing the water molecule which we propose to protonate the 3'-oxyanion leaving group (Figure 6A). The moderate effect of LNA at -1 on substrate ground state binding (Table 2) relative to 2'-OCH3 suggests that the extra methylene group causes less steric constraints than the 2'-OCH3 group; likewise, the LNA (T-1) substrate was cleaved by E. coli P RNA at the c0 site, whereas c0 cleavage was not seen at all in the presence of the 2'-OCH3 (T-1) modification (Figure 2). However, in contrast to the mild defect in substrate ground state binding, the high Mg2+ requirement for c0 cleavage of LNA (T-1) ptRNA at saturating enzyme concentrations (Figure 4E) is consistent with a severe block in the binding of catalytically relevant Mg2+. Thus, the defect is on Kconf and/or kc0 rather than on Kc0 (Scheme 1). It should also be noted that the effect of 2'-OCH3 at -1 on ground state binding (30-fold at 15 mM Ca2+; Table 2) was still moderate compared with the complete blockage of cleavage at site c0 for this modification. Thus, we favor a model according to which the extra methylene or methyl group causes a steric interference with the nearby bound catalytic Mg2+ during its re-coordination on the way to the transition state for cleavage (Figure 6). To which extent the defect is exerted at the level of Kconf or bond breakage (kc0) cannot be discerned on the basis of our data. Figure 6.Transition state models for E. coli RNase P RNA-catalyzed phosphodiester hydrolysis at the canonical c0 site with a ribose (A) or LNA (B) at nt -1. The model in (A) [adapted from ref. (55)] predicts that both MgA and MgB directly coordinate to the pro-Rp oxygen, with MgA coordinating the OH- nucleophile, and MgB interacting with the 3'-oxygen and the 2'-hydroxyl group at nt -1 via a water molecule of its hydration shell; we propose this water molecule to donate the proton to the 3'-oxyanion leaving group. The ribose at nt -1 is assumed to mainly populate the 2'-endo sugar pucker based on NMR analyses of acceptor stem mimics (26). (B) Here, the -1 nucleotide is LNA with the sugar moiety fixed in the C3'-endo conformation; the steric interference caused by LNA or 2'-OCH3 at position -1 (indicated by the dashed curved line) likely disrupts the binding site for MgB. The model differs from others [e.g. (56)] by assuming that only two instead of three metal ions are essential; also, in our model MgB is proposed to simultaneously interact with the pro-Rp oxygen, the 3'-oxygen and the 2'-OH, whereas the latter interaction is assigned to the third metal ion in the model by Kazantsev and Pace (56). Group I intron ribozymes have a similar size as bacterial P RNA and also catalyze phosphoryl transfer reactions yielding products with 3'-OH termini. The two-metal-ion mechanistic model we propose here is essentially identical to that for the second step of group I intron splicing inferred from a crystal structure of the Azoarcus sp. ptRNAIle group I intron (57), with two differences: (i) the contact of MgB to the 2'-OH is outer-sphere in the RNase P reaction, but inner-sphere in the group I intron reaction; (ii) the nucleophile is a hydroxide in the RNase P reaction, but the terminal 3'-OH of the 5'-exon in the second step of the group I intron reaction (57). Despite striking similarities, one mechanistic difference has been observed in phosphodiester hydrolysis reactions catalyzed by the Tetrahymena group I intron-derived ribozyme and E. coli P RNA: whereas a 2'-amino modification at ptRNA position -1 inhibited cleavage at the canonical site with increasing protonation of the 2'-amine in the P RNA reaction (consistent with electrostatic repulsion of a metal ion), the reverse was seen with the Tetrahymena ribozyme; in the latter reaction, protonation of the 2'-amine at equivalent position rather stimulated cleavage at the canonical site (20,58). The molecular basis of this difference is not clear at present.
###end p 69

Transition state models for E. coli RNase P RNA-catalyzed phosphodiester hydrolysis at the canonical c0 site with a ribose (A) or LNA (B) at nt -1. The model in (A) [adapted from ref. (55)] predicts that both MgA and MgB directly coordinate to the pro-Rp oxygen, with MgA coordinating the OH- nucleophile, and MgB interacting with the 3'-oxygen and the 2'-hydroxyl group at nt -1 via a water molecule of its hydration shell; we propose this water molecule to donate the proton to the 3'-oxyanion leaving group. The ribose at nt -1 is assumed to mainly populate the 2'-endo sugar pucker based on NMR analyses of acceptor stem mimics (26). (B) Here, the -1 nucleotide is LNA with the sugar moiety fixed in the C3'-endo conformation; the steric interference caused by LNA or 2'-OCH3 at position -1 (indicated by the dashed curved line) likely disrupts the binding site for MgB. The model differs from others [e.g. (56)] by assuming that only two instead of three metal ions are essential; also, in our model MgB is proposed to simultaneously interact with the pro-Rp oxygen, the 3'-oxygen and the 2'-OH, whereas the latter interaction is assigned to the third metal ion in the model by Kazantsev and Pace (56). Group I intron ribozymes have a similar size as bacterial P RNA and also catalyze phosphoryl transfer reactions yielding products with 3'-OH termini. The two-metal-ion mechanistic model we propose here is essentially identical to that for the second step of group I intron splicing inferred from a crystal structure of the Azoarcus sp. ptRNAIle group I intron (57), with two differences: (i) the contact of MgB to the 2'-OH is outer-sphere in the RNase P reaction, but inner-sphere in the group I intron reaction; (ii) the nucleophile is a hydroxide in the RNase P reaction, but the terminal 3'-OH of the 5'-exon in the second step of the group I intron reaction (57). The molecular basis of this difference is not clear at present.

###xml 1310 1319 1310 1319 <label xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Figure 7.</label>
###xml 1379 1380 1379 1380 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">3</sub>

###xml 1526 1527 1526 1527 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">3</sub>
###xml 1555 1559 1555 1559 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">endo</italic>
###xml 1575 1576 1575 1576 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">D</bold>
###xml 1319 1626 1319 1626 <p xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" textid="72">Illustration of commonalities and differences between 2&#8242;-OCH<sub>3</sub> and LNA. ###xml 1319 1626 1319 1626 <caption xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><p textid="72">Illustration of commonalities and differences between 2&#8242;-OCH<sub>3</sub> and LNA. ###xml 1626 1626 1626 1626 <graphic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xlink:href="gkp775f7"/>
###xml 1310 1626 1310 1626 <fig xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" id="F7" position="float"><label>Figure 7.</label><caption><p textid="72">Illustration of commonalities and differences between 2&#8242;-OCH<sub>3</sub> and LNA. (<bold>A</bold>) Conformation of the ribose at nt &#8722;1 assuming a preference for the C2&#8242;-<italic>endo</italic> pucker at this position (<xref ref-type="bibr" rid="B26">26</xref>); (<bold>B</bold>) for LNA; (<bold>C</bold>) for 2&#8242;-OCH<sub>3</sub>-modified ribose in the C3&#8242;-<italic>endo</italic> conformation; (<bold>D</bold>) overlay of structures shown in panel A, B and C.</p></caption><graphic xlink:href="gkp775f7"/></fig>
Despite their similar chemical nature, the effects of LNA and 2'-OCH3 substitutions at nt -1 differed in detail. Relevant towards understanding these differences could be the fact that the 2'-O-methylene group in LNA occupies a position in space that coincides only with one of the many positions that can be adopted by the 2'-OCH3 group. The latter can rotate around the C2'-O2' bond, and additional orientations result from the equilibrium between C3'- and C2'-endo sugar puckers (Figure 7). The orientation flexibility of the 2'-OCH3 group relative to the methylene group in LNA may explain the differential effects of the two substitutions on substrate ground state binding (Table 2). In addition, we recently showed that LNA at -1 stabilizes base pairing between T-1 and U73 (see Figure 1) (43). Thus, in the ground state the LNA (T-1) substrate is expected to adopt an extended acceptor stem structure to a larger degree than the 2'-OCH3 (T-1) substrate. However, based on our recent results, the breaking of an additional base pair at the end of the acceptor stem leaves the energetic barrier for reaching the transition state of the chemical step for cleavage at the canonical (c0) phosphodiester essentially unaffected (43). An effect on the aberrant m+1 and m-1 cleavage pathways cannot be excluded. Figure 7.Illustration of commonalities and differences between 2'-OCH3 and LNA. (A) Conformation of the ribose at nt -1 assuming a preference for the C2'-endo pucker at this position (26); (B) for LNA; (C) for 2'-OCH3-modified ribose in the C3'-endo conformation; (D) overlay of structures shown in panel A, B and C.
###end p 71
###begin p 72
###xml 60 61 60 61 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">3</sub>

###xml 145 149 145 149 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">endo</italic>
###xml 175 177 175 177 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B26">26</xref>
###xml 181 182 181 182 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">B</bold>
###xml 194 195 194 195 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">C</bold>

###end p 72
###begin p 73
###xml 60 67 60 67 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">E. coli</italic>
###xml 90 91 90 91 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">3</sub>
###xml 682 684 682 684 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B20">20</xref>
###xml 726 727 726 727 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">3</sub>
###xml 795 799 795 799 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">endo</italic>
###xml 960 964 960 964 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">endo</italic>
###xml 1045 1047 1045 1047 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B26">26</xref>
###xml 1088 1099 1088 1099 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">B. subtilis</italic>
###xml 1219 1226 1219 1226 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="table" rid="T2">Table 2</xref>
###xml 1294 1305 1294 1305 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">B. subtilis</italic>
###xml 1380 1381 1380 1381 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">3</sub>
###xml 1431 1442 1431 1442 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">B. subtilis</italic>
###xml 60 67 <span type="species:ncbi:562">E. coli</span>
###xml 1088 1099 <span type="species:ncbi:1423">B. subtilis</span>
###xml 1294 1305 <span type="species:ncbi:1423">B. subtilis</span>
###xml 1431 1442 <span type="species:ncbi:1423">B. subtilis</span>
Regarding metal-dependent substrate ground state binding to E. coli P RNA, 2'-H and 2'-OCH3 substituents at nt -1 were most deleterious, whereas LNA and 2'-F at this position had, overall, mild effects. We infer (i) that the absence of an H-bond donor function at the 2'-position of nt -1, as with LNA, is not crucial for substrate ground state binding; (ii) an extra 2'-methyl(ene) group has a mild effect if its position is constrained as in LNA; (iii) the mild effect of 2'-F can be explained in several ways; we favor the idea that the electronegative 2'-fluorine, as 2'-OH or LNA but in contrast to 2'-H, can accept a hydrogen bond from a metal ion-coordinated water molecule (20); this should also be the case for 2'-OCH3, but here steric interference seems to dominate; (iv) a locked C3'-endo sugar pucker at nt -1 is compatible with high affinity ground state binding, indicating that the sugar pucker at this position has not to be constrained in C2'-endo for ground state binding, a possibility that arose from our previous NMR study (26). The observed effects were similar for B. subtilis P RNA, but apart from its lower substrate affinity, this RNA was less sensitive to the 2'-deoxy substitution at nt -1 (Table 2). However, sensitivity to the 2'-deoxy modification emerged in the B. subtilis holoenzyme reaction, where the effect of 2'-H even exceeded that of 2'-OCH3. Possibly, the 2'-deoxy effect was masked in the B. subtilis P RNA-alone context owing to the generally low substrate affinity of this RNA in the absence of the protein cofactor. In summary, our results are consistent with the idea that acceptance of a hydrogen bond from a metal ion-coordinated water molecule is a key feature of the 2'-substitutent at nt -1 during substrate ground state binding.
###end p 73
###begin title 74
Cleavage fidelity
###end title 74
###begin p 75
###xml 43 46 43 46 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Gly</sup>
###xml 60 71 60 71 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">B. subtilis</italic>
###xml 77 80 77 80 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Asp</sup>
###xml 82 84 82 84 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B22">22</xref>
###xml 146 153 146 153 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">E. coli</italic>
###xml 299 306 299 306 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">E. coli</italic>
###xml 364 365 364 365 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">R</italic>
###xml 544 546 544 546 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B22">22</xref>
###xml 697 699 697 699 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B20">20</xref>
###xml 700 702 700 702 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B22">22</xref>
###xml 732 733 732 733 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">3</sub>
###xml 962 970 962 970 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F8">Scheme 1</xref>
###xml 60 71 <span type="species:ncbi:1423">B. subtilis</span>
###xml 146 153 <span type="species:ncbi:562">E. coli</span>
###xml 299 306 <span type="species:ncbi:562">E. coli</span>
For simple class I tRNAs, such as the ptRNAGly used here or B. subtilis ptRNAAsp (22), it has previously been discussed that interactions between E. coli P RNA and the ptRNA cleavage site are of redundant nature. For example, base identity switches at nt -1 that weaken the interaction with A248 of E. coli P RNA, 2'-substitutions at nt -1 (e.g. 2'-H or 2'-F), an Rp-phosphorothioate substitution at the scissile phosphodiester or a G292C or G293C mutation in the CCA-binding site of P RNA do not by themselves lead to substantial miscleavage (22). However pairwise modification, such as 2'-deoxy modification at nt -1 combined with an A248U or G292C mutation resulted in substantial miscleavage (20,22). In contrast, LNA and 2'-OCH3 at nt -1 induced severe miscleavage in the absence of any other modification, emphasizing the deleterious effect of the extra methyl(ene) group on cleavage chemistry or a preceding conformational rearrangement of E*S complexes (Scheme 1).
###end p 75
###begin title 76
###xml 21 23 21 23 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">2+</sup>
###xml 59 60 59 60 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">0</sub>
###xml 81 83 81 83 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+1</sub>
Differences in the Mg2+ and pH dependence of the correct (c0) and mis-cleavage (m+1) pathways
###end title 76
###begin p 77
###xml 207 210 207 210 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Asp</sup>
###xml 233 240 233 240 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">E. coli</italic>
###xml 277 278 277 278 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">0</sub>
###xml 281 282 281 282 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">&#8722;</sub>
###xml 282 283 282 283 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">1</sub>
###xml 301 303 301 303 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+1</sub>
###xml 310 312 310 312 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B22">22</xref>
###xml 371 372 371 372 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">0</sub>
###xml 378 379 378 379 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">&#8722;</sub>
###xml 379 380 379 380 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">1</sub>
###xml 409 411 409 411 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">2+</sup>
###xml 478 479 478 479 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">&#8722;</sub>
###xml 479 480 479 480 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">1</sub>
###xml 504 506 504 506 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">2+</sup>
###xml 582 583 582 583 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">0</sub>
###xml 632 634 632 634 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">2+</sup>
###xml 680 681 680 681 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">&#8722;</sub>
###xml 681 682 681 682 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">1</sub>
###xml 689 691 689 691 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B22">22</xref>
###xml 735 737 735 737 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+1</sub>
###xml 773 781 773 781 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F4">Figure 4</xref>
###xml 855 856 855 856 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">0</sub>
###xml 890 892 890 892 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">2+</sup>
###xml 966 968 966 968 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">2+</sup>
###xml 1041 1043 1041 1043 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+1</sub>
###xml 233 240 <span type="species:ncbi:562">E. coli</span>
Harris and coworkers recently demonstrated communication between a 2'-deoxy modification at nt -1 and weakening of the A248/nt -1 contact. Alterations at both sites, such as combination of 2'-deoxy U-1 ptRNAAsp with the A248U mutant E. coli P RNA, resulted in cleavage at the c0, m-1 and also at the m+1 site (22). When studying the proportion of cleavage events at the c0 and m-1 site as a function of the Mg2+ concentration, the authors observed increased utilization of the m-1 site with increasing Mg2+ concentration. They concluded that the pathway leading to cleavage at the c0 site exhibits a greater apparent affinity for Mg2+ than the pathway leading to cleavage at the m-1 site (22). We performed a similar analysis for the m+1 site in the presence of LNA at -1 (Figure 4) and observed a reverse correlation: here the amount of cleavage at the c0 site increased with increasing Mg2+, indicating that the LNA modification lowers the apparent affinity for Mg2+ of the canonical cleavage pathway below that of the pathway leading to m+1 cleavage.
###end p 77
###begin p 78
###xml 18 20 18 20 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B22">22</xref>
###xml 36 37 36 37 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">F</italic>

###xml 922 923 922 923 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">0</sub>
###xml 984 985 984 985 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">K</italic>

###xml 82 89 <span type="species:ncbi:562">E. coli</span>
###xml 323 327 <span type="species:ncbi:59530">kobs</span>
###xml 447 451 <span type="species:ncbi:59530">kobs</span>
###xml 577 581 <span type="species:ncbi:59530">kobs</span>
###xml 610 614 <span type="species:ncbi:59530">kobs</span>
###xml 809 813 <span type="species:ncbi:59530">kobs</span>
###xml 916 920 <span type="species:ncbi:59530">kobs</span>
###xml 1129 1133 <span type="species:ncbi:59530">kobs</span>
###xml 1140 1144 <span type="species:ncbi:59530">kobs</span>
###xml 1172 1176 <span type="species:ncbi:59530">kobs</span>
###xml 1183 1187 <span type="species:ncbi:59530">kobs</span>
###xml 2195 2202 <span type="species:ncbi:562">E. coli</span>
###xml 2463 2467 <span type="species:ncbi:59530">kobs</span>
###xml 2483 2487 <span type="species:ncbi:59530">kobs</span>
###xml 2505 2509 <span type="species:ncbi:59530">kobs</span>
The same authors (22) observed that Fc remained essentially constant over time in E. coli P RNA cleavage of ptRNAs with 2'-deoxy modifications at nt -1 and/or mutations at A248 of P RNA. Thus, they assumed that cleavages at the canonical and aberrant site (m-1 in their case) follow the same single exponential decay rate (kobs) calculated from the sum of the c0 and m+1 site cleavage products accumulating at individual time points. Using Fc and kobs, the apparent first-order rate constant for cleavage (kapp) at a particular site was calculated according to kapp[c0] = Fc x kobs, and kapp[m-1] = (1 - Fc) x kobs [for details, see ref. (22)]. Although this assumption also holds for our data obtained at Mg2+ concentrations between 60 and 200 mM (Figure 4F), it is not applicable particularly to the Fc and kobs data obtained under conditions of low [Mg2+] and low pH (see Figures 4A, F and 5A). Changes in Fc and kobs[c0] may include contributions from the equilibria described by Kc0 and Kconf[c0] (and the corresponding forward and back rate constants) as well as kc0 (see Scheme 1). Our findings (Fc increases with [Mg2+], kobs[c0] < kobs[m+1] at low [Mg2+] and pH, kobs[c0] > kobs[m+1] at high [Mg2+] and high pH) can be explained within the following framework (also illustrated in Scheme 2): When enzyme and substrate are mixed, rapid equilibria of (E*S)c0 and (E*S)m+1 form, which commit the complexes to reaction along the different pathways. Partitioning between (E*S)c0 and (E*S)m+1 is sensitive to the Mg2+ concentration. At low [Mg2+], the ratio of equilibrium binding constants Kc0/Km+1 (Scheme 1) exceeds that at higher [Mg2+], explaining why Fc increased with [Mg2+] (Figure 4A-D). The rate constant for bond breakage at the c0 site (kc0) and/or the preceding conformational step (Kconf[c0]; see Scheme 1) have a higher Mg2+ requirement than that for m+1 cleavage (Figure 4F). Finally, different steps are rate-limiting for cleavage at sites c0 and m+1, as inferred from the different pH dependencies between pH 5.5 and 7.0 (Figure 5D). Scheme 2.Illustration of the differential effects of Mg2+ concentration on the parallel pathways for cleavage of the LNA (T-1) substrate by E. coli P RNA, using a simplified version of Scheme 1. At low [Mg2+], the proportion of (E*S)c0 complexes is lower than at high [Mg2+] (indicated by differences in letter size); also, increasing [Mg2+] increases the observed rate constant for cleavage at the c0 site, kobs[c0], more than kobs[m+1]; changes in kobs may include effects on Kconf and/or the chemical step (kc0 and km+1, respectively; see Scheme 1).
###end p 78

###xml 131 138 131 138 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">E. coli</italic>
###xml 176 184 176 184 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F8">Scheme 1</xref>
###xml 196 198 196 198 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">2+</sup>
###xml 225 226 225 226 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">0</sub>
###xml 262 264 262 264 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">2+</sup>
###xml 329 331 329 331 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">2+</sup>

###xml 400 403 400 403 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">obs</sub>

###xml 533 541 533 541 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F8">Scheme 1</xref>
###xml 131 138 <span type="species:ncbi:562">E. coli</span>
###xml 399 403 <span type="species:ncbi:59530">kobs</span>
###xml 419 423 <span type="species:ncbi:59530">kobs</span>
###xml 441 445 <span type="species:ncbi:59530">kobs</span>
Illustration of the differential effects of Mg2+ concentration on the parallel pathways for cleavage of the LNA (T-1) substrate by E. coli P RNA, using a simplified version of Scheme 1. At low [Mg2+], the proportion of (E*S)c0 complexes is lower than at high [Mg2+] (indicated by differences in letter size); also, increasing [Mg2+] increases the observed rate constant for cleavage at the c0 site, kobs[c0], more than kobs[m+1]; changes in kobs may include effects on Kconf and/or the chemical step (kc0 and km+1, respectively; see Scheme 1).
